        function gptClean(gptText) {

    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="15b2cadc31364ece823f0c3ed7513c3e"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="15b2cadc31364ece823f0c3ed7513c3e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1b29ee41-56f7-4704-b0dd-5970025ed1a4'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d205223889" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A bifunctional nanoreactor of single-atom Mn supported on N-doped carbon is constructed over MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. It facilitates the reversible conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> during sodium storage by not only confining the volume change and agglomeration of discharge products but also providing catalytic Mn−N4 sites for the charging process.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/beaf122e-25ab-4bcb-bd2a-4bf9125dd19c/anie202411255-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/beaf122e-25ab-4bcb-bd2a-4bf9125dd19c/anie202411255-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5868fd7b-6ec8-41ba-bfbd-da84a095a1de/anie202411255-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/beaf122e-25ab-4bcb-bd2a-4bf9125dd19c/anie202411255-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d205223894" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Conversion-type electrode materials have gained massive research attention in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), but their limited reversibility hampers practical use. Herein, we report a bifunctional nanoreactor to boost highly reversible sodium-ion storage, wherein a record-high reversible degree of 85.65 % is achieved for MoS<sub>2</sub> anodes. Composed of nitrogen-doped carbon-supported single atom Mn (NC-SAMn), this bifunctional nanoreactor concurrently confines active materials spatially and catalyzes reaction kinetics. <i>In situ/ex situ</i> characterizations including spectroscopy, microscopy, and electrochemistry, combined with theoretical simulations containing density functional theory and molecular dynamics, confirm that the NC-SAMn nanoreactors facilitate the electron/ion transfer, promote the distribution and interconnection of discharging products (Na<sub>2</sub>S/Mo), and reduce the Na<sub>2</sub>S decomposition barrier. As a result, the nanoreactor-promoted MoS<sub>2</sub> anodes exhibit ultra-stable cycling with a capacity retention of 99.86 % after 200 cycles in the full cell. This work demonstrates the superiority of bifunctional nanoreactors with two-dimensional confined and catalytic effects, providing a feasible approach to improve the reversibility for a wide range of conversion-type electrode materials, thereby enhancing the application potential for long-cycled SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202411255-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202411255-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have achieved widespread commercial availability but are limited by the uneven distribution and the rapidly escalating cost of lithium sources.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0001_R_d205223879e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are attracting intensive attention, because of their lower cost, better temperature adaptability, and higher safety than LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0003_R_d205223879e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1b - Nat. Energy">1b</a>, <a href="#anie202411255-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0004_R_d205223879e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> However, the larger size of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02  Å <i>vs</i>. 0.76  Å of Li<sup>+</sup>) makes developing cost-effective, high-performance anode materials more challenging.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0009_R_d205223879e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">3</a></sup></span> Conversion-type transition metal chalcogenides, especially the start material of MoS<sub>2</sub>, possess layered structures that facilitate good Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0010_R_d205223879e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Moreover, MoS<sub>2</sub> has a high theoretical capacity (664 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) based on a conversion reaction of MoS<sub>2</sub>+4 Na<sup>+</sup>+4 e<sup>−</sup>→Mo+2 Na<sub>2</sub>S.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0013_R_d205223879e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> These characteristics make MoS<sub>2</sub> a promising anode material for SIBs. The high cycling capabilities and energy efficiency of conversion-type anodes of SIBs depend on their high reversible degree of conversion reaction.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0017_R_d205223879e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> However, achieving highly reversible conversion with MoS<sub>2</sub> is challenging due to severe structural pulverization during discharge and the insulating nature and high decomposition barrier of the agglomerated Na<sub>2</sub>S. As a result, an irreversible conversion reaction happens, in which Mo and S are formed instead of the original MoS<sub>2</sub> after the first cycle.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0018_R_d205223879e899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span> The irreversible conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> leads to severe electrochemical polarization and rapid capacity decay.</p>
         
         <p>To boost the reversible degree of conversion reaction of MoS<sub>2</sub>, considerable efforts have been dedicated as follows: i) incorporating MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with TiO<sub>2</sub>, carbon, or other metal compounds to reduce the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path, enhance electrical conductivity, and accommodate structural pulverization;<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0019_R_d205223879e915" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> ii) Modifying the interface of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based heterostructures to maximize the synergistic effect;<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0023_R_d205223879e921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> iii) Designing structures at the atomic level to enhance intrinsic Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and electronic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0027_R_d205223879e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, the reversible conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> should require 1) excellent electrical conductivity, 2) fast mass transport, 3) effectively encapsulating reaction mediums and buffer volume variations, 4) good chemical adsorption of discharged products, and 5) a small decomposition barrier of Na<sub>2</sub>S.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0031_R_d205223879e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> Most proposed strategies have focused on modifying pristine MoS<sub>2</sub> during discharge, whereas less attention is given to the discharged products of Mo/Na<sub>2</sub>S during charge. Therefore, these strategies struggle to meet all the above requirements and achieve a high degree of reversible conversion. The key challenge to boosting the highly reversible conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> is to go beyond the traditional strategies and synchronously meet the above five requirements.</p>
         
         <p>Notably, nanoreactors combine many features of good electrical conductivity, good encapsulation of reaction medium, good adaptability to volume change, and high availability and activity of catalytic sites,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0036_R_d205223879e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> making them promising for boosting highly reversible conversion reaction of conversion-type anodes. Inspired by this, we first designed a bifunctional nanoreactor to boost the high reversible sodium storage of MoS<sub>2</sub>. MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are coated by nitrogen-doped carbon-supported single atom Mn (MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn) nanoreactors, which not only enhance the electrical conductivity, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, reaction medium encapsulation and distribution, and volume change buffering, but also reduce the Na<sub>2</sub>S decomposition barrier. Therefore, the highly reversible conversion reaction of MoS<sub>2</sub> is demonstrated by <i>in situ/ex situ</i> electrochemical characterization technologies. In addition, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn sheets are anchored on the carbon nanotube network (MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs) to construct robust and ordered SIB anodes. The MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs show excellent long-life reversible cycling performance (239.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles with a decay rate of 0.53 % per 100 cycles), demonstrating the superiority of spatially confined and atomically catalytic effects provided by the bifunctional nanoreactors.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202411255-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202411255-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The preparation process and structural advantages of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs are illustrated in Figure S1 and Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0001">1a</a>. The morphology and composition of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and spherical aberration-corrected TEM (AC-TEM). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0001">1b</a> and Figure S2, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn nanosheets are tightly anchored on the CNT skeletons. The MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets show around 6–8 layers with a lattice spacing of 0.64 nm, corresponding to the (002) planes of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0001">1c</a>). In addition, the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are fully coated by two-dimensional (2D) amorphous carbon without the observation of metal clusters. Both the (002) planes of CNTs and the crystal planes of MoS<sub>2</sub> are also confirmed by the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0001">1d</a>). The energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) mapping images reveal the uniform distribution of C, N, S, Mo, and Mn elements throughout the structure (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0001">1e</a>). The content of Mn is quantified as 1.5 wt % by inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES), as illustrated in Table S1. The high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) images (Figures <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0001">1f</a> and 1 g) demonstrate that the MoS<sub>2</sub> is coated with amorphous carbon. Especially, there are bright dots dispersed on the surface of carbon with an average size of 1.2 Å, indicating the single-atom distributing state of Mn atoms.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411255-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c6615449-249a-4419-b64a-04059addd88a/anie202411255-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c6615449-249a-4419-b64a-04059addd88a/anie202411255-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/340557bf-2538-4b46-9181-015f291aa279/anie202411255-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c6615449-249a-4419-b64a-04059addd88a/anie202411255-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411255-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411255" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Illustrations for the fabrication of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs and the reversible conversion reaction mechanism in SIBs. (b, c) TEM images, (d) SAED pattern, (e) STEM image with corresponding EDS mapping images, and (f, g) HAADF-STEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs. The insets in (f) are the corresponding fast Fourier transform (FFT) patterns.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>For comparison, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs (without nanoreactor) and MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs (2D confined NC nanoreactor without active Mn sites) were also prepared using a similar method as described in the Supporting Information. The morphology, structure and porosity of the as-prepared samples were performed by SEM, X-ray diffraction (XRD), Raman spectra, and N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption measurements. Related discussions are presented subsequent to Figures S3–S6. Notably, the morphologies and structures of MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs, MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs and MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs are similar, indicating the coverage of 2D nanoreactors did not damage the structure of pristine MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs.</p>
         
         <p>The chemical states were explored by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS). As presented in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0002">2a</a>, in addition to the characteristic peaks of graphitic N (400.9 eV) and pyridinic N (398.4 eV), the newly appeared peak located at 399.5 eV in the N 1s spectrum of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs indicates the existence of N−Mn covalent bonds.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0041_R_d205223879e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Small">13</a></sup></span> Moreover, the binding energies of S 2p (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0002">2b</a>) and Mo 3d (Figure S7) spectra in MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs shift to smaller values compared with those in MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs, suggesting the strong interfacial interaction between MoS<sub>2</sub> and NC-SAMn. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0002">2c</a>, the high-resolution Mn 2p spectrum displays two peaks at 642.3 and 653.5 eV, assigned to positively charged Mn<sup>δ+</sup> stabilized by N atoms.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0042_R_d205223879e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> The Mn K-edge X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0002">2d</a>) reveal that the near-edge absorption of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs is positioned between MnO and MnO<sub>2</sub>, suggesting an Mn valence between +2 and +4, which corresponds with the XPS result. The phase-uncorrected Fourier transform X-ray absorption fine structure (FT-EXAFS) of Mn K-edge (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0002">2e</a>) shows a prominent peak at 1.7 Å, which is assigned to Mn−N/O scattering. The absence of Mn–Mn coordination with a scattering length of around 2.4 Å confirms the uniform distribution of single Mn atoms.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0045_R_d205223879e1105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></sup></span> Different from Mn foil, MnO and MnO<sub>2</sub>, the wavelet transform (WT) map (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0002">2f</a>) in the plots of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs shows a single peak of maximum intensity at 4.8 Å<sup>−1</sup>, representing the Mn–N coordination bond.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0049_R_d205223879e1118" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></sup></span> The least-square fitting of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0002">2g</a> and Table S2) indicates four coordinated Mn−N bonds with an average bond length of about 2.17 Å, confirming the formation of Mn-N<sub>4</sub> sites. The aforementioned analysis proves the presence of Mn-N<sub>4</sub> single-atom sites in the NC-SAMn nanoreactors.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0041_R_d205223879e1131" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Small">13</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411255-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/17a91440-0961-43d3-9694-f3dd27d56a72/anie202411255-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/17a91440-0961-43d3-9694-f3dd27d56a72/anie202411255-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f6bfcb51-cad1-448a-bf75-970119caa565/anie202411255-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/17a91440-0961-43d3-9694-f3dd27d56a72/anie202411255-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411255-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411255" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Chemical states characterizations. (a) N 1s and (b) S 2p spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs and MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs. (c) Mn 2p spectrum of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs. (d) Mn K-edge XANES spectra, (e) FT-EXAFS spectra, and (f) Wavelet transform of the k<sup>3</sup>-weighted EXAFS data of Mn, MnO, MnO<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs. (g) R space FT-EXAFS fitting curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs and the atomic structure model of Mn-N<sub>4</sub> coordination.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To probe into the impact of the 2D bifunctional nanoreactor on sodium storage, sodium-ion half cells were constructed. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests are presented in Figure S8. For the charging process, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0003">3a</a>, the curve of MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs presents a main peak at ~1.82 V and an acromial peak at ~2.22 V, which are attributed to the independent decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>S into S and Na, suggesting a large degree of irreversible conversion reaction.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0021_R_d205223879e1185" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8b - Small">8b</a>, <a href="#anie202411255-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0050_R_d205223879e1185" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Lett.">17</a></sup></span> Comparably, the main peak downshifts to 1.77 V, and the acromial peak decays in MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs and even disappears in MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs, suggesting the suppressed irreversible conversion, especially with the NC-SAMn nanoreactor. Moreover, the curve of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs shows two oxidation peaks of maximum intensity at 0.46 V and 0.71 V, corresponding to the conversion from Na<sub>2</sub>S/Mo to Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0018_R_d205223879e1201" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span> Therefore, the MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs portray the highest reversibility. Moreover, the CV curves of the following two cycles of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs overlap well, demonstrating good structural stability.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411255-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3d74e19d-30c1-4617-89e0-a9bf4e7a33f8/anie202411255-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3d74e19d-30c1-4617-89e0-a9bf4e7a33f8/anie202411255-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/649b0927-269c-405b-a36b-5e4a35b25baa/anie202411255-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3d74e19d-30c1-4617-89e0-a9bf4e7a33f8/anie202411255-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411255-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411255" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) The charging scan of CV curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs, MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs, and MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs. (b) Mo 3d XPS spectrum of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs after the 1st cycle. (c) The reversible conversion ratio of MoS<sub>2</sub> in samples. (d) Cycling performance with GDC profiles inset, (e) cycling capacity retention comparison with reported references of the MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs half cell. (f) The GDC profiles of the anode, cathode, and full cells. (g) Cycling performance of the full cell and the lighted commercial red LEDs powered by a single assembled full cell.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p><i>Ex situ</i> XPS technology was used to quantitatively evaluate the reversible conversion degree of MoS<sub>2</sub>. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0003">3b</a> and Figure S9, the Mo 3d spectra of the three samples after the initial cycle are fitted into MoS<sub>2</sub>, Mo metal, and Mo oxidizes (Mo elements oxidized by air during testing). The reversible conversion degree of MoS<sub>2</sub> after the first cycle is quantified using the ratio of the peak area of MoS<sub>2</sub> to the combined peak areas of MoS<sub>2</sub> and Mo (Table S3). Since both Mo and 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoclusters can be oxidated into Mo oxides during the sample transfer process in the air,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0051_R_d205223879e1265" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> it is difficult to distinguish the origin of Mo oxides. Therefore, we consider two scenarios: Mo oxides resulting from 100 % Mo oxidation or 100 % 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> oxidation. Under the first condition, the conversion ratios of MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs, MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs, and MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs increase from 30.53 %, 37.38 %, to 41.39 %. Under the second condition, the ratios increase from 52.76 %, 75.51 %, to 85.65 %, respectively. (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0003">3c</a>). The MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs possess the highest reversible conversion degrees in both conditions, which is consistent with the CV results.</p>
         
         <p>The cycling and rate performance of the above half cells were tested by galvanostatic discharge/charge (GDC) measurements (Figure S10). The initial discharge capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs is approximately 564.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a Coulombic efficiency of 66.6 %. The irreversible capacity in the first cycle results from the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0054_R_d205223879e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span> According to the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) results in Figure S11, MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs show the highest mass content of MoS<sub>2</sub>, which explains its high initial capacity when cycling. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure S12, the capacities of MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs increase in the initial 80 cycles and then decline rapidly to 61.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles. This initial increase is attributed to the pulverization of MoS<sub>2</sub> which generates more active sites.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0055_R_d205223879e1308" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a></sup></span> Under the protection of NC coating layers and NC-SAMn nanoreactors, the capacities of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs and MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs exhibit smaller fluctuations. Due to the highest reversible conversion degree ascribed to the NC-SAMn nanoreactor, MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs maintain the highest capacity of 239.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a decay rate of just 0.53 % per 100 cycles. Rate tests were then performed as shown in Figures S13 and S14. MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs present the highest capacity retentions under different current densities. This advantage remains evident even when compared with reported MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0003">3e</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0018_R_d205223879e1328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202411255-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0030_R_d205223879e1328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10c - ACS Nano">10c</a>, <a href="#anie202411255-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0055_R_d205223879e1328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a>, <a href="#anie202411255-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0056_R_d205223879e1328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The excellent cycling and rate performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs inspire us to explore their practical feasibility in full cells. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F/C was used as the cathode and its half-cell performance was evaluated (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0003">3f</a> and Figure S15). Considering the CV curves of both electrodes, the voltage window of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F/C‖MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs full cell was set to 0.5–4.1 V. As presented in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0003">3g</a>, the full cell shows good rate performance of 284.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and maintains 219.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 99.86 % per cycle. As shown inset Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0003">3g</a>, a full cell can easily power a series of commercial light-emitting diodes, showing its great prospects for practical application.</p>
         
         <p>The excellent sodium storage capability is attributed to good reaction kinetics. Therefore, CV tests at scanning rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> were performed to study the reaction kinetics of the above three kinds of electrodes. The normalized CV curves of samples are presented in Figure S16. Corresponding calculations were conducted in Figure S17 and described below. Based on the kinetics calculations, the capacitive contribution of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs increases with increasing the scanning rate, dominating at 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> with a high value of 88.6 % (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0004">4a</a>). The capacitive contribution values of MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs and MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs were also calculated as shown in Figure S18 and both show high values. These results demonstrate that the 2D bifunctional nanoreactor contributes to a fast electrochemical reaction kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411255-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5b8fa1ad-7304-40e5-8e19-5c23e852dcb6/anie202411255-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5b8fa1ad-7304-40e5-8e19-5c23e852dcb6/anie202411255-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f2d29611-1aa6-40b7-bccd-523fa1ab3906/anie202411255-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5b8fa1ad-7304-40e5-8e19-5c23e852dcb6/anie202411255-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411255-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411255" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) The calculated capacitive contribution of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs. (b) The fitted value of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of samples at different charging/discharging states. (c) The calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients during the discharging and charging processes. (d) <i>In situ</i> XRD patterns, (e) <i>in situ</i> Raman spectra and (f) <i>ex situ</i> TEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs in the first discharging/charging cycle. (g) Illustration of the reversible conversion reaction mechanism of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further investigate the fast reaction kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs, tests including electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were conducted. Figure S19 presents the Nyquist plots with two semicircles, which represent contact resistance (R<sub>f</sub>) and charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) in the fitted equivalent circuit (Figure S20). The R<sub>f</sub> is attributed to the formation of SEI film and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> is related to the kinetic performance of the electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0065_R_d205223879e1454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22">22</a></sup></span> As present in Table S4 and Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0004">4b</a>, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values increase during discharge and decrease during charge, which is attributed to the reversible formation and decomposition of insulating Na<sub>2</sub>S. Notably, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs is higher than the others at all tested states during the initial 10 cycles. After charging to 3.0 V in the second cycle, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs is 29.37 Ω, which is smaller than those of MoS<sub>2</sub>/CNTs (56.57 Ω) and MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs (47.94 Ω). These results indicate that profited from the 2D bifunctional nanoreactor, MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs shows the lowest resistance.</p>
         
         <p>GITT measurements were conducted with details on the setup and calculation process illustrated in Figure S21, and original data presented in Figure S22. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0004">4c</a>, MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs exhibit a higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient than MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC/CNTs, especially during the charging process, suggesting better reaction kinetics for reversible conversion.</p>
         
         <p>Besides fast reaction kinetics, the excellent capacity retention of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs was also investigated by evaluating the morphology and chemical states of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs after 20 cycles. As shown in Figure S23a, the electrode maintains a three-dimensional skeleton without severe agglomeration. Moreover, the signals of MoS<sub>2</sub> are observed in both high-resolution TEM images (Figure S23b) and S and Mo XPS spectra (Figures S24d, e), supporting the conclusion that the reversible conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> is enabled by the 2D confined NC-SAMn nanoreactor. The EDS mapping images (Figure S23c) exhibit the uniform distribution of elements and the full XPS spectrum (Figure S24a) shows the same elements, demonstrating the outstanding structural stability of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs. The C−N bonds, N-metal bonds, and combined Mn can still be respectively detected in the C1s spectrum (Figure S24b), N1s spectrum (Figure S24c), and Mn 2p spectrum (Figure S24f), demonstrating the stable electrochemical stability of the NC-SAMn nanoreactor.</p>
         
         <p>Benefiting from the 2D bifunctional nanoreactor, MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs exhibit good bidirectional reaction kinetics and strong structural durability. To investigate the impact of the NC-SAMn nanoreactor on the intrinsic irreversible conversion mechanism of MoS<sub>2</sub>, <i>in situ</i> measurements including XRD and Raman were carried out. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0004">4d</a>, during the discharge process, the diffraction peaks at around 33° and 60° corresponding to the (100) and (110) planes of MoS<sub>2</sub> gradually attenuate to disappear at 0.01 V,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0068_R_d205223879e1523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">23</a></sup></span> indicating complete conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> to Mo/Na<sub>2</sub>S. During the charging process, the aforementioned two peaks gradually reappear as the voltage increases to 3.0 V. <i>In situ</i> Raman spectra depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0004">4e</a> are consistent with the XRD results. The in-plane E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> and out-of-plane A<sub>1g</sub> vibrational modes of MoS<sub>2</sub> gradually diminish and vanish during discharge, while reappearing as the voltage increases to 3.0 V, suggesting the regeneration of MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>The phase transformation of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs was also illustrated in the <i>ex situ</i> TEM images (Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0004">4f</a>) at different states. During Na<sup>+</sup> insertion, the interlamellar spacing of layered MoS<sub>2</sub> expands, gradually forming Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> at 1.5 V (state #2). Along with the discharging process going to 0.01 V (state #3), the layered MoS<sub>2</sub> nearly disappears and Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo appear. With the voltage increasing, the discharging products react with each other and Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> reappears at 1.5 V (state #4). When the voltage comes back to 3.0 V (state #5), the layered MoS<sub>2</sub> is formed, showing the excellent reversibility of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above analysis, the reversible conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs can be described as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0004">4g</a>. In the initial state (#1), MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are confined in the NC-SAMn nanoreactor. With the insertion of Na, Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> intermediates are formed and the layered structures of MoS<sub>2</sub> are dissociated (#2). After sodiation to 0.01 V (#3), Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo particles are formed in the nanoreactor. With the extraction of Na (#4), Na<sub>2</sub>S decomposes and reacts with Mo, forming Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>. Finally, MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets re-form in the nanoreactor (#5). Notably, the confinement and catalytic effects of the bifunctional NC-SAMn nanoreactor play a crucial role in facilitating this reversible reaction.</p>
         
         <p>To gain a theoretical understanding of the reversible conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> boosted by the bifunctional NC-SAMn nanoreactor, density function theory (DFT) calculations and ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations were performed. Atomic models are constructed with detailed parameters described in the Supporting Information. As shown in Figure S25, a single-layered MoS<sub>2</sub>/N-doped graphene with a single Mn atom (MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn) is constructed and represents MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn. For comparison, a single-layered MoS<sub>2</sub>/N-doped graphene (MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG) is constructed and represents MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC.</p>
         
         <p>The electronic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier were calculated to study the reaction kinetics during discharge. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0005">5a</a>, the total and partial density of states (DOS) reveal that MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn exhibits significantly improved electrical conductivity than MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0069_R_d205223879e1635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span> Moreover, Figure S26 shows that MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn displays moderate Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy (E<sub>ad</sub>) of −1.31 eV when compared with the overly strong Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption in MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG (−3.58 eV). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths in the two kinds of interfaces are presented in Figure S27 and inserted in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0005">5b</a>. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn interface is 0.78 eV, substantially lower than that in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG interface (3.19 eV), due to the charge difference provided by Mn atoms. These results confirm that the Mn-N<sub>4</sub> sites in the nanoreactor significantly improve the electrical conductivity and facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion of MoS<sub>2</sub> during the sodiation process.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411255-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/92f536ac-aa95-45b8-ba19-11f5152dedf8/anie202411255-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/92f536ac-aa95-45b8-ba19-11f5152dedf8/anie202411255-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3d9c1374-803d-47e7-9ac9-d03964447a2a/anie202411255-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/92f536ac-aa95-45b8-ba19-11f5152dedf8/anie202411255-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411255-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411255" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Theoretical calculation results. (a) DOS and (b) Na diffusion barriers of MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn and MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG. (c) Side view for bond length and bond angle of Na<sub>2</sub>S on the charge density difference diagram of NG-SAMn and NG. The blue and yellow represent charge loss and accumulation. (d) COHP of the Mn−S bond in the Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG-SAMn system and the N−Na1 bond in the Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG system. (e) COHP of the S−Na2 bonds in the Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG-SAMn and the Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG systems. (f) The Na<sub>2</sub>S decomposition barriers on NG-SAMn and NG substrates. (g) Snapshots of trajectories for Na insertion into the MoS<sub>2</sub> and NG-SAMn/MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn during 6 ps AIMD simulations at 300 K. (h) Illustration of the reversible conversion reaction of MoS<sub>2</sub> boosted by the 2D bifunctional NG-SAMn nanoreactor. The insets of (b) and (f) represent the diffusion path of Na in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn interface and the decomposition path of NaS<sub>2</sub> on NG-SAMn, respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Upon full discharge, MoS<sub>2</sub> transforms into Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S. The Na<sub>2</sub>S adsorption, decomposition, and electronic conductivity on NG and NG-SAMn substrates were analyzed. Figure S28 illustrates the Na<sub>2</sub>S adsorbed on NG and NG-SAMn models. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0005">5c</a>, the Na<sub>2</sub>S molecules are effectively anchored on the surface of NG-SAMn and NG substrates via Mn−S and N−Na1 bonds, respectively, resulting in high adsorption energies of −1.83 eV and −2.31 eV. The S and Mn atoms in MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn show charge accumulation regions, indicating a strong interaction between SAMn and Na<sub>2</sub>S. The Na1−S−Na2 angle on the NG-SAMn substrate (143.55°) is greater than that on the NG substrate (88.60°), suggesting that Na<sub>2</sub>S decomposes more readily on the NG-SAMn substrate.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0072_R_d205223879e1742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25">25</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To further explore the catalytic activity of the nanoreactor, the DOS of the atomic orbitals of Mn, N, Na, and S atoms were analyzed. As shown in Figure S29, the Mn-3d and S-3p orbitals in Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG-SAMn exhibit significant hybridization in the range of −12.1 to −11.0 and −3.7 to −1.2 eV, corresponding to the bonding region of the crystal orbital Hamiltonian population (COHP) between the Mn−S bonds in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0005">5d</a>. However, the hybridization of N-2p and Na-3 s orbitals in Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG is not prominent, which aligns with the lower COHP of the N−Na1 bond. To directly quantify the bonding strength, the integrated COHP (ICOHP) was further calculated.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0075" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0075_R_d205223879e1755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26">26</a></sup></span> The Mn−S bond in Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG-SAMn presents a more negative ICOHP value (−1.98 eV) than the N−Na1 bond in Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG (−0.36 eV), verifying its stronger bond. Although the bond lengths of S−Na1 and S−Na2 in both systems are similar, they demonstrate different decomposition priorities. In the Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG-SAMn system, the S−Na1 and S−Na2 bonds are symmetrically distributed, indicating equal probabilities of breaking. In the Na<sub>2</sub>S/NG system, the S−Na2 bond is considered to break preferentially because the Na1 atom is hard to release, requiring the breaking of both S−Na1 and N−Na1 bonds. Therefore, the COHP of the S−Na2 bonds was examined. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0005">5e</a>, the S−Na2 bond of Na<sub>2</sub>S on the NG-SAMn substrate is weakened, evidenced by a more positive ICOHP value (−1.00 eV) compared to the NG substrate (−1.1 eV).</p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, the decomposition barriers of Na<sub>2</sub>S were studied and the decomposition pathways are presented in Figure S30 and Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0005">5f</a>. The Na<sub>2</sub>S decomposition energy barrier on the NG-SAMn substrate is 1.39 eV, which is lower than that on the NG substrate (1.62 eV). Besides, as presented in Figure S31, the electronic conductivity of NG-SAMn is better than that of NG. These results indicate that the NG-SAMn nanoreactor exhibits good catalytic activity for the key step of Na<sub>2</sub>S decomposition, thereby promoting the reversible conversion reaction between decomposed Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo to form MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>To investigate the confinement effect of the 2D NG-SAMn nanoreactor, AIMD simulations were performed with Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn, and NG-SA−Mn/MoS<sub>2</sub>/NG-SAMn models, as shown in Figure S32 and Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0005">5g</a>. In pure MoS<sub>2</sub>, the inserted Na atoms are prone to dissociate as time prolongs. When it comes to 6 ps, over 33 % of Na atoms have been released from the NaMoS<sub>2</sub> lattice, resulting in capacity fading. When the NG-SAMn layer supports one side of MoS<sub>2</sub>, it significantly inhibits the disengagement of Na atoms on the NG-SAMn side. Moreover, when both sides of MoS<sub>2</sub> interlayers are confined by NG-SAMn layers, all Na atoms remain bound within the 2D confined space, demonstrating optimal stability and capacity. It indicates that the 2D catalytic NG-SAMn nanoreactor can effectively suppress the dissociation of Na atoms, suggesting high structural stability of MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs composites.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202411255-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202411255-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Based on the experimental results and theoretical simulations, it is demonstrated that the NC-SAMn nanoreactor possesses spatially confined and atomically catalytic effects on the inner MoS<sub>2</sub>. This bifunctional nanoreactor not only modulates the interaction of reaction intermediates<span><sup><a href="#anie202411255-bib-0078" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411255-bib-0078_R_d205223879e1825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a></sup></span> at the atomic scale, but also provides a confined reaction space at the nanoscale. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411255-fig-0005">5h</a>, the NC-SAMn nanoreactor enables MoS<sub>2</sub> with (1) excellent electrical conductivity, (2) fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, (3) effective accommodation to volume variations in the discharging process, and makes discharge products (Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo) (4) good electrical conductivity, (5) uniform and tight distribution, (6) good chemical adsorption energy, (7) excellent catalytic ability to the Na<sub>2</sub>S decomposition in the charging process. Therefore, the 2D bifunctional NC-SAMn nanoreactor leads to good bidirectional conversion reaction kinetics and robust structural stability during the discharge/charge processes, endowing a large degree of reversibility of MoS<sub>2</sub> anode, thus resulting in excellent long-life cycling performance.</p>
         
         <p>In summary, the designed 2D confined and catalytic bifunctional NC-SAMn nanoreactors have successfully realized highly reversible conversion in the MoS<sub>2</sub> anode. On one hand, the nanoreactor provides effective and tight protection for the inner MoS<sub>2</sub>, enabling a spatially confined function on the volume change and agglomeration during discharge. On the other hand, the Mn-N<sub>4</sub> sites in the nanoreactor significantly enhance the Na<sub>2</sub>S adsorption, distribution, and decomposition, promoting the reversible conversion between Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo to form MoS<sub>2</sub> during charge. Impressively, MoS<sub>2</sub>@NC-SAMn/CNTs exhibit excellent long-life cycling performance of 239.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles (1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) with a decay rate of 0.53 % per 100 cycles. This work not only reports a novel type of 2D confined and catalytic bifunctional nanoreactors but also provides new insight into the design of highly reversible conversion-type anode materials toward outstanding ion-storage batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

               <ul class="rlist separator">
